THE BROTHER WHO FAILED The Monroe family be hold a Christmas reunion at the old Prince Edward Island homestead at White Sands .
It be the first time they have all be together under one roof since the death of their mother , thirty year before .
The idea of this Christmas reunion have originate with Edith Monroe the precede spring , during her tedious convalescence from a bad attack of pneumonia among stranger in an American city , where she have not be able to fill her concert engagement , and have more spare time in which to feel the tug of old tie and the homesick longing for her own people than she have have for year .
As a result , when she recover , she write to her second brother , James Monroe , who live on the homestead ; and the consequence be this gathering of the Monroes under the old roof-tree .
Ralph Monroe for once lay aside the care of his railroad , and the deceitfulness of his million , in Toronto and take the long-promised , long-deferred trip to the homeland .
Malcolm Monroe journeyed from the far western university of which he be president .
Edith come , flush with the triumph of her late and most successful concert tour .
Mrs. Woodburn , who have be Margaret Monroe , come from the Nova Scotia town where she live a busy , happy life a the wife of a rise young lawyer .
James , prosperous and hearty , greet them warmly at the old homestead whose fertile acre have well repay his skillful management .
They be a merry party , cast aside their care and year , and hark back to joyous boyhood and girlhood once more .
James have a family of rosy lad and lass ; Margaret bring her two blue-eyed little girl ; Ralph 's dark , clever-looking son accompany him , and Malcolm bring his , a young man with a resolute face , in which there be less of boyishness than in his father 's , and the eye of a keen , perhaps a hard bargainer .
The two cousin be the same age to a day , and it be a family joke among the Monroes that the stork must have mix the baby , since Ralph 's son be like Malcolm in face and brain , while Malcolm 's boy be a second edition of his uncle Ralph .
To crown all , Aunt Isabel come , too -- a talkative , clever , shrewd old lady , a young at eighty-five a she have be at thirty , think the Monroe stock the best in the world , and beamingly proud of her nephew and niece , who have go out from this humble , little farm to destiny of such brilliance and influence in the world beyond .
I have forget Robert .
Robert Monroe be apt to be forget .
Although he be the old of the family , White Sands people , in name over the various member of the Monroe family , would add , `` and Robert , '' in a tone of surprise over the remembrance of his existence .
He live on a poor , sandy little farm down by the shore , but he have come up to James ' place on the evening when the guest arrive ; they have all greet him warmly and joyously , and then do not think about him again in their laughter and conversation .
Robert sit back in a corner and listen with a smile , but he never speak .
Afterwards he have slip noiselessly away and go home , and nobody notice his go .
They be all gayly busy recall what have happen in the old time and tell what have happen in the new .
Edith recount the success of her concert tour ; Malcolm expatiate proudly on his plan for develop his beloved college ; Ralph describe the country through which his new railroad ran , and the difficulty he have have to overcome in connection with it .
James , aside , discuss his orchard and his crop with Margaret , who have not be long enough away from the farm to lose touch with it interest .
Aunt Isabel knit and smile complacently on all , talk now with one , now with the other , secretly quite proud of herself that she , an old woman of eighty-five , who have seldom be out of White Sands in her life , could discuss high finance with Ralph , and high education with Malcolm , and hold her own with James in an argument on drainage .
The White Sands school teacher , an arch-eyed , red-mouthed bit a girl -- a Bell from Avonlea -- who board with the James Monroes , amuse herself with the boy .
All be enjoy themselves hugely , so it be not to be wonder at that they do not miss Robert , who have go home early because his old housekeeper be nervous if leave alone at night .
He come again the next afternoon .
From James , in the barnyard , he learn that Malcolm and Ralph have drive to the harbor , that Margaret and Mrs. James have go to call on friend in Avonlea , and that Edith be walk somewhere in the wood on the hill .
There be nobody in the house except Aunt Isabel and the teacher .
`` You 'd better wait and stay the evening , '' say James , indifferently .
`` They 'll all be back soon . ''
Robert go across the yard and sit down on the rustic bench in the angle of the front porch .
It be a fine December evening , as mild a autumn ; there have be no snow , and the long field , slop down from the homestead , be brown and mellow .
A weird , dreamy stillness have fall upon the purple earth , the windless wood , the rain of the valley , the sere meadow .
Nature seem to have fold satisfied hand to rest , know that her long , wintry slumber be come upon her .
Out to sea , a dull , red sunset fade out into somber cloud , and the ceaseless voice of many water come up from the tawny shore .
Robert rest his chin on his hand and look across the vale and hill , where the feathery gray of leafless hardwood be mingle with the sturdy , unfailing green of the conebearers .
He be a tall , bent man , with thin , gray hair , a lined face , and deeply-set , gentle brown eye -- the eye of one who , look through pain , see rapture beyond .
He felt very happy .
He love his family clannishly , and he be rejoice that they be all again near to him .
He be proud of their success and fame .
He be glad that James have prosper so well of late year .
There be no canker of envy or discontent in his soul .
He hear absently indistinct voice at the open hall window above the porch , where Aunt Isabel be talk to Kathleen Bell .
Presently Aunt Isabel move nearer to the window , and her word come down to Robert with startle clearness .
`` Yes , I can assure you , Miss Bell , that I 'm real proud of my nephew and niece .
They 're a smart family .
They 've almost all do well , and they have n't any of them much to begin with .
Ralph have absolutely nothing and to-day he be a millionaire .
Their father meet with so many loss , what with his ill-health and the bank failing , that he could n't help them any .
But they 've all succeed , except poor Robert -- and I must admit that he 's a total failure . ''
`` Oh , no , no , '' say the little teacher deprecatingly .
`` A total failure ! ''
Aunt Isabel repeat her word emphatically .
She be not go to be contradict by anybody , least of all a Bell from Avonlea .
`` He have be a failure since the time he be bear .
He be the first Monroe to disgrace the old stock that way .
I 'm sure his brother and sister must be dreadfully ashamed of him .
He have live sixty year and he have n't do a thing worth while .
He ca n't even make his farm pay .
If he 's keep out of debt it 's as much a he 's ever manage to do . ''
`` Some men ca n't even do that , '' murmur the little school teacher .
She be really so much in awe of this imperious , clever old Aunt Isabel that it be positive heroism on her part to venture even this faint protest .
`` More be expect of a Monroe , '' say Aunt Isabel majestically .
`` Robert Monroe be a failure , and that be the only name for him . ''
Robert Monroe stand up below the window in a dizzy , uncertain fashion .
Aunt Isabel have be speak of him !
He , Robert , be a failure , a disgrace to his blood , of whom his near and dear be ashamed !
Yes , it be true ; he have never realize it before ; he have know that he could never win power or accumulate rich , but he have not think that matter much .
Now , through Aunt Isabel 's scornful eye , he saw himself a the world saw him -- a his brother and sister must see him .
THERE lay the sting .
What the world thought of him do not matter ; but that his own should think him a failure and disgrace be agony .
He moan a he start to walk across the yard , only anxious to hide his pain and shame away from all human sight , and in his eye be the look of a gentle animal which have be stricken by a cruel and unexpected blow .
Edith Monroe , who , unaware of Robert 's proximity , have be stand on the other side of the porch , saw that look , a he hurry past her , unseeing .
A moment before her dark eye have be flash with anger at Aunt Isabel 's word ; now the anger be drown in a sudden rush of tear .
She take a quick step after Robert , but check the impulse .
Not then -- and not by her alone -- could that deadly hurt be heal .
Nay , more , Robert must never suspect that she know of any hurt .
She stand and watch him through her tear a he go away across the low-lying shore field to hide his broken heart under his own humble roof .
She yearn to hurry after him and comfort him , but she know that comfort be not what Robert need now .
Justice , and justice only , could pluck out the sting , which otherwise must rankle to the death .
Ralph and Malcolm be drive into the yard .
Edith go over to them .
`` Boys , '' she say resolutely , `` I want to have a talk with you . ''
The Christmas dinner at the old homestead be a merry one .
Mrs. James spread a feast that be fit for the hall of Lucullus .
Laughter , jest , and repartee flew from lip to lip .
Nobody appear to notice that Robert eat little , say nothing , and sit with his form shrinking in his shabby `` best '' suit , his gray head bent even low than usual , a if desirous of avoid all observation .
When the others speak to him he answer deprecatingly , and shrank still far into himself .
Finally all have eat all they could , and the remainder of the plum pudding be carry out .
Robert give a low sigh of relief .
It be almost over .
Soon he would be able to escape and hide himself and his shame away from the mirthful eye of these men and woman who have earn the right to laugh at the world in which their success give them power and influence .
He -- he -- only -- be a failure .
He wonder impatiently why Mrs. James do not rise .
Mrs. James merely lean comfortably back in her chair , with the righteous expression of one who have do her duty by her fellow creature ' palate , and look at Malcolm .
Malcolm rise in his place .
Silence fell on the company ; everybody look suddenly alert and expectant , except Robert .
He still sit with bow head , wrap in his own bitterness .
`` I have be tell that I must lead off , '' say Malcolm , `` because I be suppose to possess the gift of gab .
But , if I do , I be not go to use it for any rhetorical effect to-day .
Simple , earnest word must express the deep feeling of the heart in do justice to it own .
Brothers and sister , we meet to-day under our own roof-tree , surround by the benediction of the past year .
Perhaps invisible guest be here -- the spirit of those who found this home and whose work on earth have long be finish .
It be not amiss to hope that this be so and our family circle make indeed complete .
To each one of u who be here in visible bodily presence some measure of success have fall ; but only one of u have be supremely successful in the only thing that really count -- the thing that count for eternity as well a time -- sympathy and unselfishness and self-sacrifice .
`` I shall tell you my own story for the benefit of those who have not hear it .
When I be a lad of sixteen I start to work out my own education .
Some of you will remember that old Mr. Blair of Avonlea offer me a place in his store for the summer , at wage which would go far towards pay my expense at the country academy the next winter .
I go to work , eager and hopeful .
All summer I try to do my faithful best for my employer .
In September the blow fell .
A sum of money be miss from Mr. Blair 's till .
I be suspect and discharge in disgrace .
All my neighbor believe me guilty ; even some of my own family look upon me with suspicion -- nor could I blame them , for the circumstantial evidence be strongly against me . ''
Ralph and James look ashamed ; Edith and Margaret , who have not be bear at the time refer to , lift their face innocently .
Robert do not move or glance up .
He hardly seem to be listen .
`` I be crush in an agony of shame and despair , '' continue Malcolm .
`` I believe my career be ruin .
I be bent on cast all my ambition behind me , and go west to some place where nobody know me or my disgrace .
But there be one person who believe in my innocence , who say to me , ` You shall not give up -- you shall not behave a if you be guilty .
You be innocent , and in time your innocence will be prove .
Meanwhile show yourself a man .
You have nearly enough to pay your way next winter at the Academy .
I have a little I can give to help you out .
Do n't give in -- never give in when you have do no wrong . '
`` I listen and take his advice .
I go to the Academy .
My story be there as soon a I be , and I find myself sneer at and shun .
Many a time I would have give up in despair , have it not be for the encouragement of my counselor .
He furnish the backbone for me .
I be determine that his belief in me should be justify .
I study hard and come out at the head of my class .
Then there seem to be no chance of my earn any more money that summer .
But a farmer at Newbridge , who care nothing about the character of his help , if he could get the work out of them , offer to hire me .
The prospect be distasteful but , urge by the man who believe in me , I take the place and endure the hardship .
Another winter of lonely work pass at the Academy .
I win the Farrell Scholarship the last year it be offer , and that mean an Arts course for me .
I go to Redmond College .
My story be not openly know there , but something of it get abroad , enough to taint my life there also with it suspicion .
But the year I graduate , Mr. Blair 's nephew , who , a you know , be the real culprit , confess his guilt , and I be clear before the world .
Since then my career have be what be call a brilliant one .
But '' -- Malcolm turn and lay his hand on Robert 's thin shoulder -- `` all of my success I owe to my brother Robert .
It be his success -- not mine -- and here to-day , since we have agree to say what be too often leave to be say over a coffin lid , I thank him for all he do for me , and tell him that there be nothing I be more proud of and thankful for than such a brother . ''
Robert have look up at last , amaze , bewilder , incredulous .
His face crimson a Malcolm sit down .
But now Ralph be get up .
`` I be no orator a Malcolm be , '' he quote gayly , `` but I 've get a story to tell , too , which only one of you know .
Forty year ago , when I start in life a a business man , money be n't so plentiful with me a it may be to-day .
And I need it badly .
A chance come my way to make a pile of it .
It be n't a clean chance .
It be a dirty chance .
It look square on the surface ; but , underneath , it mean trickery and roguery .
I have n't enough perception to see that , though -- I be fool enough to think it be all right .
I tell Robert what I mean to do .
And Robert saw clear through the outward sham to the real , hideous thing underneath .
He show me what it mean and he give me a preachment about a few Monroe Traditions of truth and honor .
I saw what I have be about to do a he saw it -- a all good men and true must see it .
And I vow then and there that I 'd never go into anything that I be n't sure be fair and square and clean through and through .
I 've keep that vow .
I be a rich man , and not a dollar of my money be ` tainted ' money .
But I do n't make it .
Robert really make every cent of my money .
If it have n't be for him I 'd have be a poor man to-day , or behind prison bar , a be the other men who go into that deal when I back out .
I 've get a son here .
I hope he 'll be as clever a his Uncle Malcolm ; but I hope , still more earnestly , that he 'll be as good and honorable a man a his Uncle Robert . ''
By this time Robert 's head be bent again , and his face bury in his hand .
`` My turn next , '' say James .
`` I have n't much to say -- only this .
After mother die I take typhoid fever .
Here I be with no one to wait on me .
Robert come and nurse me .
He be the most faithful , tender , gentle nurse ever a man have .
The doctor say Robert save my life .
I do n't suppose any of the rest of u here can say we have save a life . ''
Edith wipe away her tear and spring up impulsively .
`` Years ago , '' she say , `` there be a poor , ambitious girl who have a voice .
She want a musical education and her only apparent chance of obtain it be to get a teacher 's certificate and earn money enough to have her voice train .
She study hard , but her brain , in mathematics at least , be n't as good a her voice , and the time be short .
She fail .
She be lose in disappointment and despair , for that be the last year in which it be possible to obtain a teacher 's certificate without attend Queen 's Academy , and she could not afford that .
Then her old brother come to her and tell her he could spare enough money to send her to the conservatory of music in Halifax for a year .
He make her take it .
She never know till long afterwards that he have sell the beautiful horse which he love like a human creature , to get the money .
She go to the Halifax conservatory .
She win a musical scholarship .
She have have a happy life and a successful career .
And she owe it all to her brother Robert -- '' But Edith could go no far .
Her voice fail her and she sit down in tear .
Margaret do not try to stand up .
`` I be only five when my mother die , '' she sob .
`` Robert be both father and mother to me .
Never have child or girl so wise and love a guardian a he be to me .
I have never forget the lesson he teach me .
Whatever there be of good in my life or character I owe to him .
I be often headstrong and willful , but he never lose patience with me .
I owe everything to Robert . ''
Suddenly the little teacher rise with wet eye and crimson cheek .
`` I have something to say , too , '' she say resolutely .
`` You have speak for yourselves .
I speak for the people of White Sands .
There be a man in this settlement whom everybody love .
I shall tell you some of the thing he have do . ''
`` Last fall , in an October storm , the harbor lighthouse fly a flag of distress .
Only one man be brave enough to face the danger of sail to the lighthouse to find out what the trouble be .
That be Robert Monroe .
He find the keeper alone with a broken leg ; and he sail back and make -- yes , MADE the unwilling and terrified doctor go with him to the lighthouse .
I saw him when he tell the doctor he must go ; and I tell you that no man living could have set his will against Robert Monroe 's at that moment .
`` Four year ago old Sarah Cooper be to be take to the poorhouse .
She be broken-hearted .
One man take the poor , bed-ridden , fretful old creature into his home , pay for medical attendance , and wait on her himself , when his housekeeper could n't endure her tantrum and temper .
Sarah Cooper die two year afterwards , and her late breath be a benediction on Robert Monroe -- the best man God ever make .
`` Eight year ago Jack Blewitt want a place .
Nobody would hire him , because his father be in the penitentiary , and some people think Jack ought to be there , too .
Robert Monroe hire him -- and help him , and keep him straight , and get him start right -- and Jack Blewitt be a hard-working , respected young man to-day , with every prospect of a useful and honorable life .
There be hardly a man , woman , or child in White Sands who do n't owe something to Robert Monroe ! ''
As Kathleen Bell sit down , Malcolm spring up and hold out his hand .
`` Every one of u stand up and sing Auld Lang Syne , '' he cry .
Everybody stand up and join hand , but one do not sing .
Robert Monroe stand erect , with a great radiance on his face and in his eye .
His reproach have be take away ; he be crown among his kindred with the beauty and blessing of sacred yesterday .
When the singing cease Malcolm 's stern-faced son reach over and shake Robert 's hand .
`` Uncle Rob , '' he say heartily , `` I hope that when I 'm sixty I 'll be as successful a man a you . ''
`` I guess , '' say Aunt Isabel , aside to the little school teacher , a she wipe the tear from her keen old eye , `` that there 's a kind of failure that 's the best success . ''
VII .
THE RETURN OF HESTER Just at dusk , that even , I have go upstairs and put on my muslin gown .
I have be busy all day attend to the strawberry preserving -- for Mary Sloane could not be trust with that -- and I be a little tired , and think it be hardly worth while to change my dress , especially since there be nobody to see or care , since Hester be go .
Mary Sloane do not count .
But I do it because Hester would have care if she have be here .
She always like to see me neat and dainty .
So , although I be tire and sick at heart , I put on my pale blue muslin and dressed my hair .
At first I do my hair up in a way I have always like ; but have seldom worn , because Hester have disapprove of it .
It become me ; but I suddenly felt a if it be disloyal to her , so I take the puff down again and arrange my hair in the plain , old-fashioned way she have like .
My hair , though it have a good many gray thread in it , be thick and long and brown still ; but that do not matter -- nothing matter since Hester be dead and I have send Hugh Blair away for the second time .
The Newbridge people all wonder why I have not put on mourn for Hester .
I do not tell them it be because Hester have ask me not to .
Hester have never approve of mourn ; she say that if the heart do not mourn crape would not mend matter ; and if it do there be no need of the external trapping of woe .
She tell me calmly , the night before she die , to go on wear my pretty dress just a I have always wear them , and to make no difference in my outward life because of her go .
`` I know there will be a difference in your inward life , '' she say wistfully .
And oh , there be !
But sometimes I wonder uneasily , feel almost conscience-stricken , whether it be wholly because Hester have leave me -- whether it be no partly because , for a second time , I have shut the door of my heart in the face of love at her bidding .
When I have dress I go downstairs to the front door , and sit on the sandstone step under the arch of the Virginia creeper .
I be all alone , for Mary Sloane have go to Avonlea .
It be a beautiful night ; the full moon be just rise over the wooded hill , and her light fell through the poplar into the garden before me .
Through an open corner on the western side I saw the sky all silvery blue in the afterlight .
The garden be very beautiful just then , for it be the time of the rose , and ours be all out -- so many of them -- great pink , and red , and white , and yellow rose .
Hester have love rose and could never have enough of them .
Her favorite bush be grow by the step , all glory over with blossom -- white , with pale pink heart .
I gather a cluster and pin it loosely on my breast .
But my eye fill a I do so -- I felt so very , very desolate .
I be all alone , and it be bitter .
The rose , much a I love them , could not give me sufficient companionship .
I want the clasp of a human hand , and the love-light in human eye .
And then I fell to think of Hugh , though I try not to .
I have always live alone with Hester .
I do not remember our parent , who have die in my babyhood .
Hester be fifteen year old than I , and she have always seem more like a mother than a sister .
She have be very good to me and have never deny me anything I want , save the one thing that matter .
I be twenty-five before I ever have a lover .
This be not , I think , because I be more unattractive than other woman .
The Merediths have always be the `` big '' family of Newbridge .
The rest of the people look up to u , because we be the granddaughter of old Squire Meredith .
The Newbridge young men would have think it no use to try to woo a Meredith .
I have not a great deal of family pride , a perhaps I should be ashamed to confess .
I find our exalted position very lonely , and care more for the simple joy of friendship and companionship which other girl have .
But Hester possess it in a double measure ; she never allow me to associate on a level of equality with the young people of Newbridge .
We must be very nice and kind and affable to them -- noblesse oblige , a it be -- but we must never forget that we be Merediths .
When I be twenty-five , Hugh Blair come to Newbridge , have buy a farm near the village .
He be a stranger , from Lower Carmody , and so be not imbue with any preconception of Meredith superiority .
In his eye I be just a girl like others -- a girl to be woo and win by any man of clean life and honest heart .
I meet him at a little Sunday-School picnic over at Avonlea , which I attend because of my class .
I think him very handsome and manly .
He talk to me a great deal , and at last he drive me home .
The next Sunday evening he walk up from church with me .
Hester be away , or , of course , this would never have happen .
She have go for a month 's visit to distant friend .
In that month I live a lifetime .
Hugh Blair court me a the other girl in Newbridge be court .
He take me out drive and come to see me in the evening , which we spend for the most part in the garden .
I do not like the stately gloom and formality of our old Meredith parlor , and Hugh never seem to feel at ease there .
His broad shoulder and hearty laughter be oddly out of place among our fade , old-maidish furnishing .
Mary Sloane be very much please at Hugh 's visit .
She have always resent the fact that I have never have a `` beau , '' seem to think it reflect some slight or disparagement upon me .
She do all she could to encourage him .
But when Hester return and find out about Hugh she be very angry -- and grieve , which hurt me far more .
She tell me that I have forget myself and that Hugh 's visit must cease .
I have never be afraid of Hester before , but I be afraid of her then .
I yield .
Perhaps it be very weak of me , but then I be always weak .
I think that be why Hugh 's strength have appeal so to me .
I need love and protection .
Hester , strong and self-sufficient , have never felt such a need .
She could not understand .
Oh , how contemptuous she be .
I tell Hugh timidly that Hester do not approve of our friendship and that it must end .
He take it quietly enough , and go away .
I think he do not care much , and the thought selfishly make my own heartache worse .
I be very unhappy for a long time , but I try not to let Hester see it , and I do n't think she do .
She be not very discern in some thing .
After a time I get over it ; that be , the heartache cease to ache all the time .
But thing be never quite the same again .
Life always seem rather dreary and empty , in spite of Hester and my rose and my Sunday-School .
I suppose that Hugh Blair would find him a wife elsewhere , but he do not .
The year go by and we never meet , although I saw him often at church .
At such time Hester always watch me very closely , but there be no need of her to do so .
Hugh make no attempt to meet me , or speak with me , and I would not have permit it if he have .
But my heart always yearn after him .
I be selfishly glad he have not marry , because if he have I could not have think and dream of him -- it would have be wrong .
Perhaps , a it be , it be foolish ; but it seem to me that I must have something , if only foolish dream , to fill my life .
At first there be only pain in the thought of him , but afterwards a faint , misty little pleasure crept in , like a mirage from a land of lose delight .
Ten year slip away thus .
And then Hester die .
Her illness be sudden and short ; but , before she die , she ask me to promise that I would never marry Hugh Blair .
She have not mention his name for year .
I think she have forget all about him .
`` Oh , dear sister , be there any need of such a promise ? ''
I ask , weep .
`` Hugh Blair do not want to marry me now .
He never will again . ''
`` He have never marry -- he have not forget you , '' she say fiercely .
`` I could not rest in my grave if I think you would disgrace your family by marry beneath you .
Promise me , Margaret . ''
I promise .
I would have promise anything in my power to make her die pillow easier .
Besides , what do it matter ?
I be sure that Hugh would never think of me again .
She smile when she hear me , and press my hand .
`` Good little sister -- that be right .
You be always a good girl , Margaret -- good and obedient , though a little sentimental and foolish in some way .
You be like our mother -- she be always weak and loving .
I take after the Merediths . ''
She do , indeed .
Even in her coffin her dark , handsome feature preserve their expression of pride and determination .
Somehow , that last look of her dead face remain in my memory , blot out the real affection and gentleness which her living face have almost always show me .
This distress me , but I could not help it .
I wish to think of her a kind and loving , but I could remember only the pride and coldness with which she have crush out my new-born happiness .
Yet I felt no anger or resentment towards her for what she have do .
I know she have mean it for the best -- my best .
It be only that she be mistake .
And then , a month after she have die , Hugh Blair come to me and ask me to be his wife .
He say he have always love me , and could never love any other woman .
All my old love for him reawaken .
I want to say yes -- to feel his strong arm about me , and the warmth of his love enfolding and guard me .
In my weakness I yearn for his strength .
But there be my promise to Hester -- that promise give by her deathbed .
I could not break it , and I tell him so .
It be the hard thing I have ever do .
He do not go away quietly this time .
He plead and reason and reproach .
Every word of his hurt me like a knife-thrust .
But I could not break my promise to the dead .
If Hester have be live I would have brave her wrath and her estrangement and go to him .
But she be dead and I could not do it .
Finally he go away in grief and anger .
That be three week ago -- and now I sit alone in the moonlit rose-garden and wept for him .
But after a time my tear dry and a very strange feeling come over me .
I felt calm and happy , a if some wonderful love and tenderness be very near me .
And now come the strange part of my story -- the part which will not , I suppose , be believe .
If it be not for one thing I think I should hardly believe it myself .
I should feel tempt to think I have dream it .
But because of that one thing I know it be real .
The night be very calm and still .
Not a breath of wind stir .
The moonshine be the bright I have ever see .
In the middle of the garden , where the shadow of the poplar do not fall , it be almost as bright a day .
One could have read fine print .
There be still a little rise glow in the west , and over the airy bough of the tall poplars one or two large , bright star be shin .
The air be sweet with a hush of dream , and the world be so lovely that I hold my breath over it beauty .
Then , all at once , down at the far end of the garden , I saw a woman walking .
I think at first that it must be Mary Sloane ; but , a she cross a moonlit path , I saw it be not our old servant 's stout , homely figure .
This woman be tall and erect .
Although no suspicion of the truth come to me , something about her reminded me of Hester .
Even so have Hester like to wander about the garden in the twilight .
I have see her thus a thousand time .
I wonder who the woman could be .
Some neighbor , of course .
But what a strange way for her to come !
She walk up the garden slowly in the poplar shade .
Now and then she stoop , a if to caress a flower , but she pluck none .
Half way up she out in to the moonlight and walk across the plot of grass in the center of the garden .
My heart give a great throb and I stand up .
She be quite near to me now -- and I saw that it be Hester .
I can hardly say just what my feeling be at this moment .
I know that I be not surprise .
I be frighten and yet I be not frighten .
Something in me shrink back in a sickening terror ; but I , the real I , be not frighten .
I know that this be my sister , and that there could be no reason why I should be frighten of her , because she love me still , a she have always do .
Further than this I be not conscious of any coherent thought , either of wonder or attempt at reasoning .
Hester pause when she come to within a few step of me .
In the moonlight I saw her face quite plainly .
It wear an expression I have never before see on it -- a humble , wistful , tender look .
Often in life Hester have look lovingly , even tenderly , upon me ; but always , a it be , through a mask of pride and sternness .
This be go now , and I felt nearer to her than ever before .
I know suddenly that she understand me .
And then the half-conscious awe and terror some part of me have felt vanish , and I only realize that Hester be here , and that there be no terrible gulf of change between u .
Hester beckon to me and say , `` Come . ''
I stand up and follow her out of the garden .
We walk side by side down our lane , under the willow and out to the road , which lay long and still in that bright , calm moonshine .
I felt a if I be in a dream , move at the bidding of a will not my own , which I could not have dispute even if I have wish to do so .
But I do not wish it ; I have only the feeling of a strange , boundless content .
We go down the road between the growth of young fir that border it .
I smell their balsam a we pass , and notice how clearly and darkly their pointed top come out against the sky .
I hear the tread of my own foot on little twig and plant in our way , and the trail of my dress over the grass ; but Hester move noiselessly .
Then we go through the Avenue -- that stretch of road under the apple tree that Anne Shirley , over at Avonlea , call `` The White Way of Delight . ''
It be almost dark here ; and yet I could see Hester 's face just as plainly a if the moon be shin on it ; and whenever I look at her she be always look at me with that strangely gentle smile on her lip .
Just a we pass out of the Avenue , James Trent overtake u , drive .
It seem to me that our feeling at a give moment be seldom what we would expect them to be .
I simply felt annoy that James Trent , the most notorious gossip in Newbridge , should have see me walk with Hester .
In a flash I anticipate all the annoyance of it ; he would talk of the matter far and wide .
But James Trent merely nod and call out , `` Howdy , Miss Margaret .
Taking a moonlight stroll by yourself ?
Lovely night , ai n't it ? ''
Just then his horse suddenly swerve , a if startle , and break into a gallop .
They whirl around the curve of the road in an instant .
I felt relieve , but puzzle .
JAMES TRENT HAD NOT SEEN HESTER .
Down over the hill be Hugh Blair 's place .
When we come to it , Hester turn in at the gate .
Then , for the first time , I understand why she have come back , and a blinding flash of joy break over my soul .
I stop and look at her .
Her deep eye gaze into mine , but she do not speak .
We go on .
Hugh 's house lay before u in the moonlight , grow over by a tangle of vine .
His garden be on our right , a quaint spot , full of old-fashioned flower grow in a sort of disorderly sweetness .
I tread on a bed of mint , and the spice of it float up to me like the incense of some strange , sacred , solemn ceremonial .
I felt unspeakably happy and bless .
When we come to the door Hester say , `` Knock , Margaret . ''
I rap gently .
In a moment , Hugh open it .
Then that happen by which , in after day , I be to know that this strange thing be no dream or fancy of mine .
Hugh look not at me , but past me .
`` Hester ! ''
he exclaim , with human fear and horror in his voice .
He lean against the door-post , the big , strong fellow , tremble from head to foot .
`` I have learn , '' say Hester , `` that nothing matter in all God 's universe , except love .
There be no pride where I have be , and no false ideal . ''
Hugh and I look into each other 's eye , wondering , and then we know that we be alone .
VIII .
THE LITTLE BROWN BOOK OF MISS EMILY The first summer Mr. Irving and Miss Lavendar -- Diana and I could never call her anything else , even after she be marry -- be at Echo Lodge after their marriage , both Diana and I spend a great deal of time with them .
We become acquaint with many of the Grafton people whom we have not know before , and among others , the family of Mr. Mack Leith .
We often go up to the Leiths in the evening to play croquet .
Millie and Margaret Leith be very nice girl , and the boy be nice , too .
Indeed , we like every one in the family , except poor old Miss Emily Leith .
We try hard enough to like her , because she seem to like Diana and me very much , and always want to sit with u and talk to u , when we would much rather have be somewhere else .
We often felt a good deal of impatience at these time , but I be very glad to think now that we never show it .
In a way , we felt sorry for Miss Emily .
She be Mr. Leith 's old-maid sister and she be not of much importance in the household .
But , though we felt sorry for her , we could n't like her .
She really be fussy and meddlesome ; she like to poke a finger into every one 's pie , and she be not at all tactful .
Then , too , she have a sarcastic tongue , and seem to feel bitter towards all the young folk and their love affair .
Diana and I think this be because she have never have a lover of her own .
Somehow , it seem impossible to think of lover in connection with Miss Emily .
She be short and stout and pudgy , with a face so round and fat and red that it seem quite featureless ; and her hair be scanty and gray .
She walk with a waddle , just like Mrs. Rachel Lynde , and she be always rather short of breath .
It be hard to believe Miss Emily have ever be young ; yet old Mr. Murray , who live next door to the Leiths , not only expect u to believe it , but assure u that she have be very pretty .
`` THAT , at least , be impossible , '' say Diana to me .
And then , one day , Miss Emily die .
I 'm afraid no one be very sorry .
It seem to me a most dreadful thing to go out of the world and leave not one person behind to be sorry because you have go .
Miss Emily be dead and bury before Diana and I hear of it at all .
The first I know of it be when I come home from Orchard Slope one day and find a queer , shabby little black horsehair trunk , all stud with brass nail , on the floor of my room at Green Gables .
Marilla tell me that Jack Leith have bring it over , and say that it have belong to Miss Emily and that , when she be die , she ask them to send it to me .
`` But what be in it ?
And what be I to do with it ? ''
I ask in bewilderment .
`` There be nothing say about what you be to do with it .
Jack say they do n't know what be in it , and have n't look into it , see that it be your property .
It seem a rather queer proceeding -- but you 're always get mixed up in queer proceeding , Anne .
As for what be in it , the easy way to find out , I reckon , be to open it and see .
The key be tie to it .
Jack say Miss Emily say she want you to have it because she love you and saw her lost youth in you .
I guess she be a bit delirious at the last and wander a good deal .
She say she want you ` to understand her . ' ''
I run over to Orchard Slope and ask Diana to come over and examine the trunk with me .
I have n't receive any instruction about keep it content secret and I know Miss Emily would n't mind Diana know about them , whatever they be .
It be a cool , gray afternoon and we get back to Green Gables just a the rain be begin to fall .
When we go up to my room the wind be rise and whistle through the bough of the big old Snow Queen outside of my window .
Diana be excite , and , I really believe , a little bit frighten .
We open the old trunk .
It be very small , and there be nothing in it but a big cardboard box .
The box be tie up and the knot seal with wax .
We lift it out and untied it .
I touch Diana 's finger a we do it , and both of u exclaim at once , `` How cold your hand be ! ''
In the box be a quaint , pretty , old-fashioned gown , not at all fade , make of blue muslin , with a little darker blue flower in it .
Under it we find a sash , a yellowed feather fan , and an envelope full of withered flower .
At the bottom of the box be a little brown book .
It be small and thin , like a girl 's exercise book , with leaf that have once be blue and pink , but be now quite fade , and stain in place .
On the fly leaf be write , in a very delicate hand , `` Emily Margaret Leith , '' and the same writing cover the first few page of the book .
The rest be not write on at all .
We sit there on the floor , Diana and I , and read the little book together , while the rain thud against the window pane .
June 19 , 18 -- I come to-day to spend a while with Aunt Margaret in Charlottetown .
It be so pretty here , where she live -- and ever so much nice than on the farm at home .
I have no cow to milk here or pig to fee .
Aunt Margaret have give me such a lovely blue muslin dress , and I be to have it make to wear at a garden party out at Brighton next week .
I never have a muslin dress before -- nothing but ugly print and dark woolen .
I wish we be rich , like Aunt Margaret .
Aunt Margaret laugh when I say this , and declare she would give all her wealth for my youth and beauty and light-heartedness .
I be only eighteen and I know I be very merry but I wonder if I be really pretty .
It seem to me that I be when I look in Aunt Margaret 's beautiful mirror .
They make me look very different from the old crack one in my room at home which always twist my face and turn me green .
But Aunt Margaret spoil her compliment by tell me I look exactly a she do at my age .
If I think I 'd ever look a Aunt Margaret do now , I do n't know what I 'd do .
She be so fat and red .
June 29 .
Last week I go to the garden party and I meet a young man call Paul Osborne .
He be a young artist from Montreal who be board over at Heppoch .
He be the handsome man I have ever see -- very tall and slender , with dreamy , dark eye and a pale , clever face .
I have not be able to keep from think about him ever since , and to-day he come over here and ask if he could paint me .
I felt very much flatter and so pleased when Aunt Margaret give him permission .
He say he want to paint me a `` Spring , '' stand under the poplar where a fine rain of sunshine fall through .
I be to wear my blue muslin gown and a wreath of flower on my hair .
He say I have such beautiful hair .
He have never see any of such a real pale gold .
Somehow it seem even pretty than ever to me since he praise it .
I have a letter from home to-day .
Ma say the blue hen steal her nest and come off with fourteen chicken , and that pa have sell the little spotted calf .
Somehow those thing do n't interest me like they once do .
July 9 .
The picture be come on very well , Mr. Osborne say .
I know he be make me look far too pretty in it , although her persists in say he ca n't do me justice .
He be go to send it to some great exhibition when finish , but he say he will make a little water-color copy for me .
He come every day to paint and we talk a great deal and he read me lovely thing out of his book .
I do n't understand them all , but I try to , and he explain them so nicely and be so patient with my stupidity .
And he say any one with my eye and hair and coloring do not need to be clever .
He say I have the sweet , merry laugh in the world .
But I will not write down all the compliment he have pay me .
I dare say he do not mean them at all .
In the evening we stroll among the spruce or sit on the bench under the acacia tree .
Sometimes we do n't talk at all , but I never find the time long .
Indeed , the minute just seem to fly -- and then the moon will come up , round and red , over the harbor and Mr. Osborne will sigh and say he suppose it be time for him to go .
July 24 .
I be so happy .
I be frighten at my happiness .
Oh , I do n't think life could ever be so beautiful for me a it be !
Paul love me !
He tell me so to-night a we walk by the harbor and watch the sunset , and he ask me to be his wife .
I have care for him ever since I meet him , but I be afraid I be not clever and well-educated enough for a wife for Paul .
Because , of course , I 'm only an ignorant little country girl and have live all my life on a farm .
Why , my hand be quite rough yet from the work I 've do .
But Paul just laugh when I say so , and take my hand and kiss them .
Then he look into my eye and laugh again , because I could n't hide from him how much I love him .
We be to be marry next spring and Paul say he will take me to Europe .
That will be very nice , but nothing matter so long a I be with him .
Paul 's people be very wealthy and his mother and sister be very fashionable .
I be frighten of them , but I do not tell Paul so because I think it would hurt him and oh , I would n't do that for the world .
There be nothing I would n't suffer if it would do him any good .
I never think any one could feel so .
I use to think if I love anybody I would want him to do everything for me and wait on me a if I be a princess .
But that be not the way at all .
Love make you very humble and you want to do everything yourself for the one you love .
August 10 .
Paul go home to-day .
Oh , it be so terrible !
I do n't know how I can bear to live even for a little while without him .
But this be silly of me , because I know he have to go and he will write often and come to me often .
But , still , it be so lonesome .
I do n't cry when he leave me because I want him to remember me smile in the way he like best , but I have be cry ever since and I ca n't stop , no matter how hard I try .
We have have such a beautiful fortnight .
Every day seem dearer and happy than the last , and now it be end and I feel a if it could never be the same again .
Oh , I be very foolish -- but I love him so dearly and if I be to lose his love I know I would die .
August 17 .
I think my heart be dead .
But no , it ca n't be , for it ache too much .
Paul 's mother come here to see me to-day .
She be not angry or disagreeable .
I would n't have be so frightened of her if she have be .
As it be , I felt that I could n't say a word .
She be very beautiful and stately and wonderful , with a low , cold voice and proud , dark eye .
Her face be like Paul 's but without the loveableness of his .
She talk to me for a long time and she say terrible thing -- terrible , because I know they be all true .
I seem to see everything through her eye .
She say that Paul be infatuate with my youth and beauty but that it would not last and what else I to give him ?
She say Paul must marry a woman of his own class , who could do honor to his fame and position .
She say that he be very talented and have a great career before him , but that if he marry me it would ruin his life .
I saw it all , just a she explain it out , and I tell her at last that I would not marry Paul , and she might tell him so .
But she smile and say I must tell him myself , because he would not believe any one else .
I could have beg her to spare me that , but I know it would be of no use .
I do not think she have any pity or mercy for any one .
Besides , what she say be quite true .
When she thank me for be so REASONABLE I tell her I be not do it to please her , but for Paul 's sake , because I would not spoil his life , and that I would always hate her .
She smile again and go away .
Oh , how can I bear it ?
I do not know any one could suffer like this !
August 18 .
I have do it .
I write to Paul to-day .
I know I must tell him by letter , because I could never make him believe it face to face .
I be afraid I could not even do it by letter .
I suppose a clever woman easily could , but I be so stupid .
I write a great many letter and tear them up , because I felt sure they would n't convince Paul .
At last I get one that I think would do .
I know I must make it seem a if I be very frivolous and heartless , or he would never believe .
I spell some word wrong and put in some mistake of grammar on purpose .
I tell him I have just be flirt with him , and that I have another fellow at home I like well .
I say FELLOW because I know it would disgust him .
I say that it be only because he be rich that I be tempt to marry him .
I think would my heart would break while I be write those dreadful falsehood .
But it be for his sake , because I must not spoil his life .
His mother tell me I would be a millstone around his neck .
I love Paul so much that I would do anything rather than be that .
It would be easy to die for him , but I do n't see how I can go on living .
I think my letter will convince Paul .
I suppose it convince Paul , because there be no further entry in the little brown book .
When we have finish it the tear be run down both our face .
`` Oh , poor , dear Miss Emily , '' sob Diana .
`` I 'm so sorry I ever think her funny and meddlesome . ''
`` She be good and strong and brave , '' I say .
`` I could never have be as unselfish a she be . ''
I think of Whittier 's line , `` The outward , wayward life we see The hidden spring we may not know . ''
At the back of the little brown book we find a faded water-color sketch of a young girl -- such a slim , pretty little thing , with big blue eye and lovely , long , ripple golden hair .
Paul Osborne 's name be write in faded ink across the corner .
We put everything back in the box .
Then we sit for a long time by my window in silence and thought of many thing , until the rainy twilight come down and blot out the world .
IX .
SARA 'S WAY The warm June sunshine be come down through the tree , white with the virginal bloom of apple-blossoms , and through the shining pane , make a tremulous mosaic upon Mrs. Eben Andrews ' spotless kitchen floor .
Through the open door , a wind , fragrant from long wandering over orchard and clover meadow , drift in , and , from the window , Mrs. Eben and her guest could look down over a long , misty valley slop to a sparkle sea .
Mrs. Jonas Andrews be spend the afternoon with her sister-in-law .
She be a big , sonsy woman , with full-blown peony cheek and large , dreamy , brown eye .
When she have be a slim , pink-and-white girl those eye have be very romantic .
Now they be so out of keep with the rest of her appearance a to be ludicrous .
Mrs. Eben , sit at the other end of the small tea-table that be draw up against the window , be a thin little woman , with a very sharp nose and light , fade blue eye .
She look like a woman whose opinion be always very decided and warrant to wear .
`` How do Sara like teach at Newbridge ? ''
ask Mrs. Jonas , help herself a second time to Mrs. Eben 's matchless black fruit cake , and thereby bestow a subtle compliment which Mrs. Eben do not fail to appreciate .
`` Well , I guess she like it pretty well -- good than down at White Sands , anyway , '' answer Mrs. Eben .
`` Yes , I may say it suit her .
Of course it 's a long walk there and back .
I think it would have be wise for her to keep on boarding at Morrison 's , a she do all winter , but Sara be bind to be home all she can .
And I must say the walk seem to agree with her . ''
`` I be down to see Jonas ' aunt at Newbridge last night , '' say Mrs. Jonas , `` and she say she 'd hear that Sara have make up her mind to take Lige Baxter at last , and that they be to be marry in the fall .
She ask me if it be true .
I say I do n't know , but I hop to mercy it be .
Now , be it , Louisa ? ''
`` Not a word of it , '' say Mrs. Eben sorrowfully .
`` Sara have n't any more notion of take Lige than ever she have .
I 'm sure it 's not MY fault .
I 've talk and argue till I 'm tired .
I declare to you , Amelia , I be terribly disappointed .
I 'd set my heart on Sara 's marrying Lige -- and now to think she wo n't ! ''
`` She be a very foolish girl , '' say Mrs. Jonas , judicially .
`` If Lige Baxter be n't good enough for her , who be ? ''
`` And he 's so well off , '' say Mrs. Eben , `` and do such a good business , and be well speak of by every one .
And that lovely new house of his at Newbridge , with bay window and hardwood floor !
I 've dream and dream of see Sara there a mistress . ''
`` Maybe you 'll see her there yet , '' say Mrs. Jonas , who always take a hopeful view of everything , even of Sara 's contrariness .
But she felt discouraged , too .
Well , she have do her best .
If Lige Baxter 's broth be spoil it be not for lack of cook .
Every Andrews in Avonlea have be try for two year to bring about a match between him and Sara , and Mrs. Jonas have bear her part valiantly .
Mrs. Eben 's despondent reply be cut short by the appearance of Sara herself .
The girl stand for a moment in the doorway and look with a faintly amuse air at her aunt .
She know quite well that they have be discuss her , for Mrs. Jonas , who carry her conscience in her face , look guilty , and Mrs. Eben have not be able wholly to banish her aggrieved expression .
Sara put away her book , kiss Mrs. Jonas ' rosy cheek , and sit down at the table .
Mrs. Eben bring her some fresh tea , some hot roll , and a little jelly-pot of the apricot preserve Sara like , and she cut some more fruit cake for her in moist plummy slice .
She might be out of patience with Sara 's `` contrariness , '' but she spoil and pet her for all that , for the girl be the very core of her childless heart .
Sara Andrews be not , strictly speak , pretty ; but there be that about her which make people look at her twice .
She be very dark , with a rich , dusky sort of darkness , her deep eye be velvety brown , and her lip and cheek be crimson .
She eat her roll and preserve with a healthy appetite , sharpen by her long walk from Newbridge , and tell amuse little story of her day 's work that make the two old woman shake with laughter , and exchange shy glance of pride over her cleverness .
When tea be over she pour the remain content of the cream jug into a saucer .
`` I must fee my pussy , '' she say a she leave the room .
`` That girl beat me , '' say Mrs. Eben with a sigh of perplexity .
`` You know that black cat we 've have for two year ?
Eben and I have always make a lot of him , but Sara seem to have a dislike to him .
Never a peaceful nap under the stove could he have when Sara be home -- out he must go .
Well , a little spell ago he get his leg break accidentally and we think he 'd have to be kill .
But Sara would n't hear of it .
She get splint and set his leg just a knacky , and bandage it up , and she have tend him like a sick baby ever since .
He 's just about well now , and he live in clover , that cat do .
It 's just her way .
There 's them sick chickens she 's be doctor for a week , give them pill and thing !
`` And she think more of that wretched-looking calf that get poison with paris green than of all the other stock on the place . ''
As the summer wear away , Mrs. Eben try to reconcile herself to the destruction of her air castle .
But she scold Sara considerably .
`` Sara , why do n't you like Lige ?
I 'm sure he be a model young man . ''
`` I do n't like model young men , '' answer Sara impatiently .
`` And I really think I hate Lige Baxter .
He have always be hold up to me a such a paragon .
I 'm tired of hear about all his perfection .
I know them all off by heart .
He do n't drink , he do n't smoke , he do n't steal , he do n't tell fibs , he never lose his temper , he do n't swear , and he go to church regularly .
Such a faultless creature a that would certainly get on my nerve .
No , no , you 'll have to pick out another mistress for your new house at the Bridge , Aunt Louisa . ''
When the apple tree , that have be pink and white in June , be russet and bronze in October , Mrs. Eben have a quilting .
The quilt be of the `` Rising Star '' pattern , which be consider in Avonlea to be very handsome .
Mrs. Eben have intend it for part of Sara 's `` set out , '' and , while she sew the red-and-white diamond together , she have regale her fancy by imagine she saw it spread out on the spare-room bed of the house at Newbridge , with herself lay her bonnet and shawl on it when she go to see Sara .
Those bright vision have fade with the apple blossom , and Mrs. Eben hardly have the heart to finish the quilt at all .
The quilting come off on Saturday afternoon , when Sara could be home from school .
All Mrs. Eben 's particular friend be range around the quilt , and tongue and finger fly .
Sara flit about , help her aunt with the supper preparation .
She be in the room , get the custard dish out of the cupboard , when Mrs. George Pye arrive .
Mrs. George have a genius for be late .
She be later than usual to-day , and she look excited .
Every woman around the `` Rising Star '' felt that Mrs. George have some news worth listen to , and there be an expectant silence while she pull out her chair and settle herself at the quilt .
She be a tall , thin woman with a long pale face and liquid green eye .
As she look around the circle she have the air of a cat daintily lick it chop over some titbit .
`` I suppose , '' she say , `` that you have hear the news ? ''
She know perfectly well that they have not .
Every other woman at the frame stop quilt .
Mrs. Eben come to the door with a pan of puffy , smoking-hot soda biscuit in her hand .
Sara stop count the custard dish , and turn her ripely-colored face over her shoulder .
Even the black cat , at her foot , cease preen his fur .
Mrs. George felt that the undivided attention of her audience be hers .
`` Baxter Brothers have fail , '' she say , her green eye shoot out flash of light .
`` Failed DISGRACEFULLY ! ''
She pause for a moment ; but , since her hearer be a yet speechless from surprise , she go on .
`` George come home from Newbridge , just before I leave , with the news .
You could have knock me down with a feather .
I should have think that firm be as steady a the Rock of Gibraltar !
But they 're ruin -- absolutely ruin .
Louisa , dear , can you find me a good needle ? ''
`` Louisa , dear , '' have set her biscuit down with a sharp thud , reckless of result .
A sharp , metallic tinkle sound at the closet where Sara have strike the edge of her tray against a shelf .
The sound seem to loosen the paralyzed tongue , and everybody begin talk and exclaim at once .
Clear and shrill above the confusion rise Mrs. George Pye 's voice .
`` Yes , indeed , you may well say so .
It IS disgraceful .
And to think how everybody trust them !
George will lose considerable by the crash , and so will a good many folk .
Everything will have to go -- Peter Baxter 's farm and Lige 's grand new house .
Mrs. Peter wo n't carry her head so high after this , I 'll be bind .
George saw Lige at the Bridge , and he say he look dreadful cut up and ashamed . ''
`` Who , or what 's to blame for the failure ? ''
ask Mrs. Rachel Lynde sharply .
She do not like Mrs. George Pye .
`` There be a dozen different story on the go , '' be the reply .
`` As far a George could make out , Peter Baxter have be speculate with other folk ' money , and this be the result .
Everybody always suspect that Peter be crook ; but you 'd have think that Lige would have keep him straight .
HE have always such a reputation for saintliness . ''
`` I do n't suppose Lige know anything about it , '' say Mrs. Rachel indignantly .
`` Well , he 'd ought to , then .
If he be n't a knave he 's a fool , '' say Mrs. Harmon Andrews , who have formerly be among his warm partisan .
`` He should have keep watch on Peter and find out how the business be be run .
Well , Sara , you be the level-headest of u all -- I 'll admit that now .
A nice mess it would be if you be marry or engage to Lige , and him leave without a cent -- even if he can clear his character ! ''
`` There be a good deal of talk about Peter , and swindling , and a lawsuit , '' say Mrs. George Pye , quilt industriously .
`` Most of the Newbridge folk think it 's all Peter 's fault , and that Lige be n't to blame .
But you ca n't tell .
I dare say Lige be as deep in the mire a Peter .
He be always a little too good to be wholesome , I think . ''
There be a clink of glass at the cupboard , a Sara set the tray down .
She come forward and stand behind Mrs. Rachel Lynde 's chair , rest her shapely hand on that lady 's broad shoulder .
Her face be very pale , but her flashing eye seek and face defiantly Mrs. George Pye 's cat-like orb .
Her voice quiver with passion and contempt .
`` You 'll all have a fling at Lige Baxter , now that he 's down .
You could n't say enough in his praise , once .
I 'll not stand by and hear it hint that Lige Baxter be a swindler .
You all know perfectly well that Lige be as honest a the day , if he IS so unfortunate a to have an unprincipled brother .
You , Mrs. Pye , know it good than any one , yet you come here and run him down the minute he 's in trouble .
If there 's another word say here against Lige Baxter I 'll leave the room and the house till you 're go , every one of you . ''
She flash a glance around the quilt that cow the gossip .
Even Mrs. George Pye 's eye flicker and wan and quail .
Nothing more be say until Sara have pick up her glass and march from the room .
Even then they dare not speak above a whisper .
Mrs. Pye , alone , smart from snub , venture to ejaculate , `` Pity save u ! ''
a Sara slam the door .
For the next fortnight gossip and rumor hold high carnival in Avonlea and Newbridge , and Mrs. Eben grow to dread the sight of a visitor .
`` They 're bind to talk about the Baxter failure and criticize Lige , '' she deplore to Mrs. Jonas .
`` And it rile Sara up so terrible .
She use to declare that she hat Lige , and now she wo n't listen to a word against him .
Not that I say any , myself .
I 'm sorry for him , and I believe he 's do his best .
But I ca n't stop other people from talk . ''
One evening Harmon Andrews come in with a fresh budget of news .
`` The Baxter business be pretty near wind up at last , '' he say , a he light his pipe .
`` Peter have get his lawsuit settle and have hush up the talk about swindling , somehow .
Trust him for slip out of a scrape clean and clever .

I hear he 's go out West in the Spring , to take up land in Alberta and try his hand at farming .
Best thing he can do , I guess .
Folks hereabouts have have enough of the Baxter breed .
Newbridge will be well rid of them . ''
Sara , who have be sit in the dark corner by the stove , suddenly stand up , let the black cat slip from her lap to the floor .
Mrs. Eben glance at her apprehensively , for she be afraid the girl be go to break out in a tirade against the complacent Harmon .
But Sara only walk fiercely out of the kitchen , with a sound a if she be struggle for breath .
In the hall she snatch a scarf from the wall , flung open the front door , and rush down the lane in the chill , pure air of the autumn twilight .
Her heart be throb with the pity she always felt for bruise and bait creature .
On and on she go heedlessly , intent only on walk away her pain , over gray , brood field and wind slope , and along the skirt of ruinous , dusky pine wood , curtain with fine spun purple gloom .
Her dress brush against the brittle grass and sere fern , and the moist night wind , loose from wild place far away , blow her hair about her face .
At last she come to a little rustic gate , lead into a shadowy wood-lane .
The gate be bind with willow withe , and , a Sara fumble vainly at them with her chill hand , a man 's firm step come up behind her , and Lige Baxter 's hand close over her 's .
`` Oh , Lige ! ''
she say , with something like a sob .
He open the gate and draw her through .
She leave her hand in his , a they walk through the lane where lissome bough of young sapling flick against their head , and the air be wildly sweet with the woodsy odor .

Lige look wistfully down at her through the gloom .
`` Yes , it seem very long to me , Sara .
But I do n't think you 'd care to see me , after what you say last spring .
And you know thing have be go against me .
People have say hard thing .
I 've be unfortunate , Sara , and may be too easy-going , but I 've be honest .
Do n't believe folk if they tell you I be n't . ''
`` Indeed , I never do -- not for a minute ! ''
fire Sara .
`` I 'm glad of that .
I 'm go away , later on .
I felt bad enough when you refuse to marry me , Sara ; but it 's well that you do n't .
I 'm man enough to be thankful my trouble do n't fall on you . ''
Sara stop and turn to him .
Beyond them the lane open into a field and a clear lake of crocus sky cast a dim light into the shadow where they stand .
Above it be a new moon , like a gleaming silver scimitar .
Sara saw it be over her left shoulder , and she saw Lige 's face above her , tender and trouble .
`` Lige , '' she say softly , `` do you love me still ? ''
`` You know I do , '' say Lige sadly .
That be all Sara want .
With a quick movement she nestle into his arm , and lay her warm , tear-wet cheek against his cold one .
When the amazing rumor that Sara be go to marry Lige Baxter , and go out West with him , circulate through the Andrews clan , hand be lift and head be shake .
Mrs. Jonas puffed and pant up the hill to learn if it be true .
She find Mrs. Eben stitch for dear life on an `` Irish Chain '' quilt , while Sara be sew the diamond on another `` Rising Star '' with a martyr-like expression on her face .
Sara hat patchwork above everything else , but Mrs. Eben be mistress up to a certain point .
`` You 'll have to make that quilt , Sara Andrews .
If you 're go to live out on those prairie , you 'll need pile of quilt , and you shall have them if I sew my finger to the bone .
But you 'll have to help make them . ''
And Sara have to .
When Mrs. Jonas come , Mrs. Eben send Sara off to the post-office to get her out of the way .
`` I suppose it 's true , this time ? ''
say Mrs. Jonas .
`` Yes , indeed , '' say Mrs. Eben briskly .
`` Sara be set on it .
There be no use try to move her -- you know that -- so I 've just conclude to make the best of it .
I 'm no turn-coat .

`` I 'm real glad you take that view of it , Louisa .

`` Well , we might have expect it , '' say Mrs. Eben sagely .
`` It be always Sara 's way .
When any creature get sick or unfortunate she seem to take it right into her heart .
So you may say Lige Baxter 's failure be a success after all . ''
X .
THE SON OF HIS MOTHER Thyra Carewe be wait for Chester to come home .
She sit by the west window of the kitchen , look out into the gathering of the shadow with the expectant immovability that characterize her .
She never twitch or fidget .
Into whatever she do she put the whole force of her nature .
If it be sit still , she sit still .
`` A stone image would be twitchedly beside Thyra , '' say Mrs. Cynthia White , her neighbor across the lane .
`` It get on my nerve , the way she sit at that window sometimes , with no more motion than a statue and her great eye burn down the lane .
When I read the commandment , ` Thou shalt have no other god before me , ' I declare I always think of Thyra .
She worship that son of hers far ahead of her Creator .
She 'll be punish for it yet . ''
Mrs. White be watch Thyra now , knit furiously , a she watch , in order to lose no time .
Thyra 's hand be fold idly in her lap .
She have not move a muscle since she sit down .
Mrs. White complain it give her the weeps .
`` It do n't seem natural to see a woman sit so still , '' she say .
`` Sometimes the thought come to me , ` what if she 's have a stroke , like her old Uncle Horatio , and be sit there stone dead ! ' ''
The evening be cold and autumnal .
There be a fiery red spot out at sea , where the sun have set , and , above it , over a chill , clear , saffron sky , be reef of purple-black cloud .
The river , below the Carewe homestead , be livid .
Beyond it , the sea be dark and brooding .
It be an evening to make most people shiver and forebode an early winter ; but Thyra love it , a she love all stern , harshly beautiful thing .
She would not light a lamp because it would blot out the savage grandeur of sea and sky .
It be well to wait in the darkness until Chester come home .
He be late to-night .
She think he have be detain over-time at the harbor , but she be not anxious .
He would come straight home to her as soon a his business be complete -- of that she felt sure .
Her thought go out along the bleak harbor road to meet him .
She could see him plainly , come with his free stride through the sandy hollow and over the windy hill , in the harsh , cold light of that forbid sunset , strong and handsome in his comely youth , with her own deeply cleft chin and his father 's dark gray , straightforward eye .
No other woman in Avonlea have a son like hers -- her only one .
In his brief absences she yearn after him with a maternal passion that have in it something of physical pain , so intense be it .
She think of Cynthia White , knit across the road , with contemptuous pity .
That woman have no son -- nothing but pale-faced girl .
Thyra have never want a daughter , but she pity and despise all sonless woman .
Chester 's dog whine suddenly and piercingly on the doorstep outside .
He be tire of the cold stone and want his warm corner behind the stove .
Thyra smile grimly when she hear him .
She have no intention of let him in .
She say she have always dislike dog , but the truth , although she would not glance at it , be that she hat the animal because Chester love him .
She could not share his love with even a dumb brute .
She love no living creature in the world but her son , and fiercely demand a like concentrated affection from him .
Hence it please her to hear his dog whine .
It be now quite dark ; the star have begin to shine out over the shorn harvest field , and Chester have not come .

They make Thyra conscious of her exceed loneliness .
She have just decide that she would walk down the lane and wait for Chester on the bridge , when a thunderous knock come at the east kitchen door .
She recognize August Vorst 's knock and light a lamp in no great haste , for she do not like him .
He be a gossip and Thyra hat gossip , in man or woman .
But August be privilege .
She carry the lamp in her hand , when she go to the door , and it upward-striking light give her face a ghastly appearance .
She do not mean to ask August in , but he push past her cheerfully , not wait to be invite .
He be a midget of a man , lame of foot and hunched of back , with a white , boyish face , despite his middle age and deep-set , malicious black eye .
He pull a crumpled newspaper from his pocket and hand it to Thyra .
He be the unofficial mail-carrier of Avonlea .
Most of the people give him a trifle for bring their letter and paper from the office .
He earn small sum in various other way , and so contrived to keep the life in his stunted body .
There be always venom in August 's gossip .
It be say that he make more mischief in Avonlea in a day than be make otherwise in a year , but people tolerate him by reason of his infirmity .
To be sure , it be the tolerance they give to inferior creature , and August felt this .
Perhaps it account for a good deal of his malignity .
He hat most those who be kindest to him , and , of these , Thyra Carewe above all .
He hat Chester , too , a he hat strong , shapely creature .
His time have come at last to wind them both , and his exultation shone through his crook body and pinched feature like an illuminating lamp .
Thyra perceive it and vaguely felt something antagonistic in it .
She point to the rocking-chair , a she might have point out a mat to a dog .
August crawl into it and smile .
He be go to make her writhe presently , this woman who look down upon him a some venomous creep thing she disdain to crush with her foot .
`` Did you see anything of Chester on the road ? ''
ask Thyra , give August the very opening he desire .
`` He go to the harbor after tea to see Joe Raymond about the loan of his boat , but it 's the time he should be back .
I ca n't think what keep the boy . ''
`` Just what keep most men -- leave out creature like me -- at some time or other in their life .
A girl -- a pretty girl , Thyra .
It please me to look at her .
Even a hunchback can use his eye , eh ?
Oh , she 's a rare one ! ''
`` What be the man talk about ? ''
say Thyra wonderingly .
`` Damaris Garland , to be sure .
Chester 's down at Tom Blair 's now , talk to her -- and look more than his tongue say , too , of that you may be sure .
Well , well , we be all young once , Thyra -- all young once , even crook little August Vorst .
Eh , now ? ''
`` What do you mean ? ''
say Thyra .
She have sit down in a chair before him , with her hand fold in her lap .

You know well enough , Thyra . ''

-- Damaris Garland a if they be something to each other .

There 's no need to look like that about it .
Young men will be young men to the end of time , and there 's no harm in Chester 's liking to look at a lass , eh , now ?
Or in talk to her either ?

He 's not so ill-looking for a man , Thyra . ''

Then she throw herself face downward on the sofa and burst into wild tear .
Her very soul ache .

But , after a time , tear fail her , and she begin bitterly to go over , word by word , what August Vorst have say .
That her son should ever cast eye of love on any girl be something Thyra have never think about .
She would not believe it possible that he should love any one but herself , who love him so much .
And now the possibility invade her mind a subtly and coldly and remorselessly a a sea-fog steal landward .
Chester have be bear to her at an age when most woman be let their child slip from them into the world , with some natural tear and heartache , but content to let them go , after enjoy their sweet year .
Thyra 's late-come motherhood be all the more intense and passionate because of it very lateness .
She have be very ill when her son be bear , and have lain helpless for long week , during which other woman have tend her baby for her .
She have never be able to forgive them for this .
Her husband have die before Chester be a year old .
She have lay their son in his dying arm and receive him back again with a last benediction .
To Thyra that moment have something of a sacrament in it .
It be a if the child have be doubly give to her , with a right to him solely that nothing could take away or transcend .
Marrying !
She have never think of it in connection with him .
He do not come of a marrying race .
His father have be sixty when he have marry her , Thyra Lincoln , likewise well on in life .
Few of the Lincolns or Carewes have marry young , many not at all .
And , to her , Chester be her baby still .
He belong solely to her .
And now another woman have dare to look upon him with eye of love .
Damaris Garland !
Thyra now remembered see her .
She be a new-comer in Avonlea , have come to live with her uncle and aunt after the death of her mother .
Thyra have meet her on the bridge one day a month previously .
Yes , a man might think she be pretty -- a low-browed girl , with a wave of reddish-gold hair , and crimson lip blossom out against the strange , milk-whiteness of her skin .
Her eye , too -- Thyra recall them -- hazel in tint , deep , and laughter-brimmed .

And to-night , while she , his mother , wait for him in darkness and loneliness , he be down at Blair 's , talk to this girl !

The thought be more bitter than death to Thyra .
That she should dare !
Her anger be all against the girl .

What do you suppose she be do down there at this hour of the night ? ''

`` He ai n't home yet .

She 'd be a liable to fly at me , tooth and claw , a not . ''

cry Cynthia .
`` Thyra Carewe be best left alone , if she be in a tantrum .
She 's like no other woman in Avonlea -- or out of it .
